Reverting to Original Claim When Sulh Consideration Is Taken
Ruling on istihqaq of sulh consideration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on istihqaq of the consideration in a sulh with acknowledgment and distinguish between the remedies when the consideration is a specific property ('ayn) versus a fungible (mithli).

Core Idea
If the sulh consideration is taken by a rightful owner, the plaintiff reverts to his original claim; if it is fungible, he demands its equivalent.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a common scenario: a plaintiff settles a claim with the defendant for a payment that later turns out to belong to a third party. The Hanafi school provides a nuanced remedy depending on the nature of the consideration, ensuring justice for all parties.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage deals with a sulh (settlement) with acknowledgment (iqrar) where the consideration (badal sulh) is later proven to belong to another person (mustahiq).
2
State the general rule
The plaintiff returns to his original claim (da‘wa) – i.e., he reasserts the right he had given up in the sulh – because he abandoned his claim only in exchange for receiving the consideration.
This applies when the consideration is property (mal) and the sulh was not made as a sale.
3
Distinguish between 'ayn and dayn
If the consideration is a specific property (dhāt al-qīma), the plaintiff reverts to the original claim. If it is a fungible debt (mithli like dirhams), the sulh remains valid and the plaintiff claims the equivalent (mithl) from the defendant.
The rationale: istihqaq invalidates receipt (istifā’), so for fungibles the sulh is intact but delivery is defective.
4
Understand the legal rationale (ta'lil)
The plaintiff gave up his claim only in consideration for the specified object. When that object is taken, the basis for the surrender is lost, so the original claim revives. For fungibles, the obligation can be satisfied with a substitute.
This ta'lil underpins the Hanafi distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قـولـه ولـو استحق الْمَصَالِحَ عليه عن اقرار .. اور اگر مصالح علیہ یعنی بدل صلح میں کسی کا استحقاق ثابت ہو جائے تو مدعی اپنے د عوی کی طرف رجوع کرے یعنی پھر سے دعویٰ دائر کرے اگر تمام بدل صلح دوسرے کا ثابت ہو تو کل مصالح عنہ کا دعوی کرے اور بعض ہو تو بعض کا کیونکہ مدعی نے جو اپنے دعوئی کو ترک کیا ہے وہ اس لئے کیا ہے تا کہ اس کے قبضہ میں بدل صلح صحیح سالم آئے۔ اور جب وہ دوسرے کا ٹھہرا تو وہ مبدل یعنی دعوئی کی طرف رجوع کرلے گا۔ مگر یہ اس وقت ہے جب بدل صلح مال میں ہو اور اس نے صلح کو جائز نہ کھا ہو۔ اور اگر ا نے صلح کو جائز نہ بکا کانال بین مدعی کے حوا۔۔ کرے اور مستحق مدعی علیہ پر اس کی قیمت میں رجوع کرے اگر وہ ذات القیم میں سے ہو اور اگر بدل صلح دین ہو۔ جیسے درا ہم ودنا نیر اور تکمیل موزوان (بغیر اعیانہا ) تو استحقاق کی وجہ سے صلح باطل نہ ہوگی۔ لیکن اس کے مثل کے ساتھ رجوع کرلے گا۔ کیونکہ استحقاق کی وجہ سے صلح باطل نہ ہوگی۔ لیکن اس کے مثل کے ساتھ رجوع کرے گا۔ کیونکہ استحقاق کی وجہ سے استیفاء باطل ہو جاتا ہے۔ پس گویا اس نے ابھی پورا حق وصول ہی نہیں کیا۔ کذافي شرح الطحاوی (کفایه)
His statement: 'And if the muṣālaḥ ‘alayhi (the consideration) is subject to istihqaq with acknowledgment...' – And if the muṣālaḥ ‘alayhi, i.e., the consideration (badal sulh), is proven to belong to someone else, then the plaintiff returns to his claim (da‘wa) – meaning he re-files the suit. If the entire consideration is proven for another, he claims the entire original subject matter (muṣālaḥ ‘anhu); if part, he claims that part. Because the plaintiff abandoned his claim only so that the consideration would come into his possession sound and entire. When it turns out to belong to another, he reverts to the original claim. But this is when the consideration is property (mal) and he did not make the sulh jā’iz (i.e., not a sale?). And if he did not make the sulh jā’iz... and the mustahiq has recourse to the defendant for its value if it is a thing of value (dhāt al-qīma). And if the consideration is a debt (dayn) like dirhams and dinars and weighed/sold items (without their exact identity), then the sulh does not become invalid due to istihqaq; rather, he recovers its equivalent (mithl). Because istihqaq invalidates the receipt (istifā’), so it is as if he has not yet received the full right. So in Sharh al-Tahawi (Kifayah).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the scenario where the consideration (badal sulh) given by the defendant to the plaintiff is later proven to belong to a third party. The rule: the plaintiff may return to his original claim (i.e., reassert the right he had given up in the sulh). This applies when the sulh was with acknowledgment. The reasoning: he gave up his claim only to receive the consideration; if the consideration is taken, the basis for the surrender is gone. Important conditions: (1) The consideration must be property (mal), not a benefit or act. (2) The sulh was not made specifically as a sale (i.e., using the word 'sale'). (3) If the consideration is a specific item (ayn), and it is taken, the plaintiff reverts to his claim; the mustahiq may claim its value from the defendant. (4) If the consideration is fungible (mithlī) like dirhams, the istihqaq does not invalidate the sulh; instead, the plaintiff claims the like (mithl) from the defendant, because the sulh remains valid but the delivery is defective. The commentary cites Sharh al-Tahawi (Kifayah) as source.

Key Terms
بدل صلح (badal ṣulḥ): The consideration given in a sulh contract.
مصلح عليه (muṣālaḥ ‘alayhi): The consideration in sulh (sometimes used interchangeably with badal).
ذات القيم (dhāt al-qīma): A non-fungible property that has value but no standard equivalent (e.g., a house, animal).
مثلي (mithlī): Fungible property that can be replaced by an exact equivalent (e.g., dirhams, wheat).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a sulh with acknowledgment, the consideration is a specific gold coin which is later proven to belong to a third party. What is the correct remedy according to the Hanafi school?
A) The sulh is void and the plaintiff must return the coin to the third party
B) The plaintiff claims the value of the coin from the defendant
C) The plaintiff reverts to his original claim against the defendant
D) The plaintiff claims a similar gold coin from the defendant

Key Takeaway
When the consideration in a sulh with acknowledgment is taken by its rightful owner, the Hanafi school allows the plaintiff to revert to the original claim, except when the consideration is fungible, where the plaintiff claims its equivalent.

Claim for Unspecified Right in a House: Partial Istihqaq
Ruling when a claim is for an unspecified right in a house and part of the house is later claimed by another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when part of a house is proven to belong to another after an unspecified sulh, and explain the rational basis for not returning any consideration.

Core Idea
In an unspecified claim, partial istihqaq does not trigger a refund because the claim may still be satisfied by the remainder.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced scenario: a person claims an unspecified right in a house, settles through sulh, and then a third party proves ownership of part of the house. The ruling clarifies whether the original claimant must return part of the consideration. The answer hinges on the ambiguity (ibham) of the original claim and the possibility that it pertains to the unclaimed portion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled on: a claim of an unspecified right, followed by sulh, then partial istihqaq. The ruling is that the claimant does not return any of the consideration.
This is the central ruling.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a rational argument (dalil 'aqli) based on possibility: the unspecified claim could have been for the remaining part, so the consideration remains valid.
No direct Quran or hadith is cited; reasoning from the nature of ambiguity.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The unspecified nature (ibham) allows the claim to be connected to any part of the house. When only part is taken, the claim may still attach to the rest, preserving the sulh.
This distinguishes it from a specified claim.
4
Note the contrast with full istihqaq
If the entire house is claimed, the consideration is totally devoid of any corresponding right, so the full consideration is returned. This is analogous to sales.
The contrast highlights the condition of partial vs. complete loss.
5
Connect to the sales analogy
The text explicitly analogizes to the rule in sales (buyu‘), where complete istihqaq of the sold item requires full refund of the price.
The principle is consistent across transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِنِ ادَّعَى حَقًّا فِي دَارٍ وَلَمْ يُبَيِّنَهُ فَصُولِحَ مِنْ ذلِكَ ثُمَّ اسْتَحَقَّ بَعْضَ الدَّارِ لَمْ يَرُدُّ شَيْئًا مِنَ الْعِوَضِ لِاَنَّ دَعْوَاهُ يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ فِيمَا بَقِيَ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا اسْتَحَقَ كُلَّهُ لِأَنَّهُ يَعْرَى الْعِوَضُ عِنْدَ ذَلِكَ عَنْ شَيْءٍ يُقَابِلُهُ فَرَجَعَ بِكُلِه عَلَى مَا قَدَّمْنَاهُ فِي الْبُيُوعِ. ترجمہ: کسی نے دعوی کیا دار میں اپنے حق کا اور اس کی تعین نہیں کی۔ پس اس سے صلح کرلی گئی۔ پھر کچھ دار کا حقدار نکل آیا۔ تو مدعی عوض میں سے کچھ واپس نہیں کرے گا۔ کیونکہ ممکن ہے اس کا دعوی باقی ماندہ میں ہو۔ بخلاف اس کے جب پورا گھر مستحق نکل آئے۔ کیونکہ اس وقت خالی ہوگا عوض ایسی چیز سے جو اس کے مقابل ہو۔ لہذا پورا عوض واپس لے گا۔ جیسا کہ ہم پہلے بیان کر چکے بیوع میں۔
He said: And if he claimed a right in a house and did not specify it, then sulh was made from that, then part of the house was claimed (mustahaqq), he does not return anything from the consideration, because his claim may possibly be in what remains. Contrary to when the entire house is claimed, because then the consideration is completely devoid of anything in exchange, so he returns all of it, as we have explained earlier in sales. Translation: Someone claimed a right in a house without specifying it. Then sulh was made. Then a part of the house's rightful owner appeared. So the plaintiff will not return anything from the consideration. Because it is possible that his claim is in the remaining part. Contrary to when the entire house is claimed; because then the consideration will be empty of anything in exchange, so he will return the entire consideration, as we have explained earlier in sales.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with ta’lil. It deals with a situation where a person claims an unspecified right in a house, makes sulh for a consideration, and then part of the house is proven to belong to a third party. The rule: the plaintiff does not have to return any of the consideration for the taken part. The reasoning: since the claim was unspecified (mubham), it is possible that the claim was only for the remaining part, not the taken part. Therefore, the consideration may still be fully matched to the claim. This is contrasted with the case where the entire house is claimed by the mustahiq; then the consideration has no corresponding right, so it must be returned in full, analogous to the rule in sales (if the entire sold item is taken, the price is refunded). The principle is that in bay‘, similar rule applies. The commentary notes that this is consistent with earlier sections on sales.

Key Terms
حق مبهم (haqq mubham): A right claimed without specifying its exact nature or location in the property.
صلح (sulh): A settlement or compromise in which a claimant accepts consideration in lieu of litigation.
استحقاق (istihqaq): The proven rightful ownership or entitlement of a third party over property that was claimed by another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person claims 'I have a right in this house' without specifying, then makes sulh, and later part of the house is proven to belong to someone else, what must the plaintiff do?
A) Return proportional consideration for the taken part
B) Return the entire consideration
C) Return nothing of the consideration
D) Re-file the claim for the taken part

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, an unspecified claim in a sulh protects the claimant from refund obligations upon partial third-party claims, as long as the claim could still apply to the remaining property.

Invalid Sulh on Part of a Claimed House and Its Remedies
Ruling: Invalid partial sulh with corrective solutions and an intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the defect in a partial sulh of a claimed property and apply the two valid corrective measures under Hanafi law.

Core Idea
A partial sulh on a claimed specific property is invalid unless you add consideration or include a release from the remaining claim.

Sulh (compromise) is a noble contract that settles disputes, but when a plaintiff claims a specific house and the parties agree on a sulh for only a part of it without adjusting the terms, the Hanafi school holds the agreement invalid. Why? Because the plaintiff takes part of his own right and remains on his claim for the rest. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the two valid ways to fix such a partial settlement, and an internal Hanafi disagreement on whether the plaintiff can later sue for the remainder.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the initial ruling
Recognize the scenario: plaintiff claims a specific house, then makes sulh with the defendant for only a piece (e.g. one room). The text states the sulh is not valid (lam yasihh). Determine the hukm level: initially batil.
The ruling is based on the logical defect: the plaintiff is settling for part of his own right without addressing the rest.
2
Locate the ‘illah (reason) for invalidity
The author gives the reason: 'because what he took possession of is part of his own right, and he remains on his claim for the rest.' The sulh fails to settle the entire dispute; it merely transfers part of the claimed property without a proper exchange for the remainder.
The ‘illah is the partiality of the settlement — the sulh must either cover the whole claim or be supplemented.
3
Analyze the two corrective solutions
The text offers two ways to make the sulh valid: (1) add a dirham to the consideration so that the total becomes compensation for the right in the remaining part, or (2) include a clause of bara'ah (release/acquittal) from the claim on the rest. Understand that these convert the partial settlement into a full settlement.
The first solution adds a nominal amount (dirham) to make it a complete exchange; the second uses a release to extinguish the claim, which is valid even though releasing the specific right is not.
4
Trace the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on hearing the later claim
The commentary discusses a dispute: if the plaintiff later sues for the rest of the house after an uncorrected partial sulh, will the court hear his claim? Sheikh Najm al-Din al-Nasafi says yes, because ibra’ min al-‘ayn (release from specific property) is invalid, so the claim remains. Sheikh al-Islam and the Nihayah say no, because the ibra’ is from the claim (da‘wa), which is valid.
This is an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf — both positions are from Hanafi scholars.
5
Evaluate the tarjih and the mufta bihi
The Nihayah declares the view that the later claim is not heard as the Zahir al-Riwayah, i.e., the most authoritative transmission. Therefore, the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that the plaintiff cannot sue again; the ibra’ from the claim is effective. Understand the weight: Zahir al-Riwayah takes precedence over a single scholar’s opinion.
The tarjih authority is Sahib al-Nihayah (likely al-Marghinani), who upholds the ruling from the six core books of Imam Muhammad.

وَلَوِ ادَّعَى دَارًا فَصَالَحَ عَلَى قِطْعَةٍ مِنْهَا لَمْ يَصِحُ الْصُّلْحُ لَانَّ مَا قَبَضَهُ عَيْنِ حَقِهِ وَهُوَ عَلَى دَعْوَاهُ فِي الْبَاقِي وَالْوَجْهُ فِيهِ اَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ إِمَّا أَنْ يَزِيدَ دِرْهَمًا فِي بَدَلِ الصُّلْحِ فَيَصِيرُ ذلِكَ عِوَضًا عَنْ حَقِهِ فِيمَا بَقِيَ أَوْ يَلْحَقَ بِهِ ذِكْرُ الْبَرَاءَةِ عَنْ دَعْوَى الْبَاقِي. ترجمہ: اگر دعوی کیا دار کا پس صلح کر لی اس کے ایک قطعہ پر تو صلح صحیح نہ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ مدعی نے جس پر قبضہ کیا ہے وہ اس کے عین حق میں سے ہے اور وہ باقی میں اپنے دعوئی پر ہے اور اس کے جواز میں دو باتوں میں سے ایک ہے یا تو بدل صلح میں ایک درہم بڑھا دے تا کہ یہ اس کے باقی دار کے حق کا عوض ہو جائے یا عوض کے ساتھ باقی دار کے دعوئی سے برأت کا ذکر لاحق کر دے۔ تشریح قوله و لوادعی دارا .... اگر مدعی نے دار کا دعویٰ کیا اور مدعلی علیہ نے اس کے ایک قطعہ پر صلح کر لی۔ مثلاً اس کے کسی ایک معین کرے پر تو صلح صحیح نہ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ مدعی نے جو کچھ قبضہ کیا ہے وہ اس کے عین حق میں سے بعض ہے اور باقی دار میں وہ اپنے دعوئی پر باقی ہے۔ پھر اگر مدعی باقی دار کے دعوئی پر بعینہ قائم کرے تو مسموع ہوگا یا نہیں۔ سوشیخ نجم الدین نسفی نے شرح کافی میں کہا ہے کہ مسموع ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس نے اپنا بعض حق وصول پایا ہے۔ اور باقی سے ابراء کیا ہے اور ابراء من العین باطل ہے۔ تو اس کا وجود و عدم برابر ہوا۔ اور شیخ الاسلام نے اپنی شرح میں ذکر کیا ہے کہ مسموع نہ ہوگا۔ اس کو صاحب نہایہ نے ظاہر الروایہ کہا ہے۔ وجہ یہ ہے کہ ابراء مین اور دعوئی دونوں سے ملاتی ہے اور ابراء عن الدعوئی صحیح ہے۔ گوابراء عن العین باطل ہے۔
And if he claimed a specific house and made sulh on a piece of it, the sulh is not valid, because what he took possession of is part of his own right, and he remains on his claim for the rest. The proper course in it is one of two: either he adds a dirham to the consideration so that it becomes compensation for his right in the remaining part, or he attaches to it the mention of acquittal (bara’ah) from the claim for the rest. Translation: If he claimed a house and then made sulh on one piece of it, the sulh is not valid. Because what the plaintiff took possession of is part of his very right, and he is still on his claim for the rest. To make it valid, one of two things: either he increases the consideration by one dirham so that it becomes compensation for his right in the remaining part, or he adds to the consideration a clause of acquittal from the claim for the rest. Commentary: Explanation of his statement: 'And if he claimed a house...' If the plaintiff claimed a house and the defendant made sulh on one piece of it, for example on a specific room, then the sulh is not valid. Because what the plaintiff took possession of is part of his own right, and he remains on his claim for the rest of the house. Then, if the plaintiff pursues the claim for the rest, will it be heard (accepted)? Sheikh Najm al-Din al-Nasafi said in Sharh al-Kafi: It will be heard, because he received part of his right and he has granted ibra' (release) for the rest, but ibra' from a specific right is invalid, so its existence and non-existence are equal. And Sheikh al-Islam [likely al-Marghinani] mentioned in his commentary that it will not be heard; Sahib al-Nihayah said this is the Zahir al-Riwayah. The reason: because ibra' (acquittal) attaches to both [the claim and the right] and ibra' from the claim is valid, even though ibra' from the specific right is invalid.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf). It states that if a plaintiff claims a specific house and then makes a sulh with the defendant for only a part of that house (e.g. one room), the sulh contract is invalid in its initial form. The reason: the plaintiff has taken part of his own right (ayn haqq), but he still has a claim on the remainder. This is not a proper sulh because a sulh should either settle the entire claim or involve a proper exchange. To validly settle only part, two solutions are given: (1) increase the consideration by an additional amount (e.g. one dirham) so that the total consideration compensates for the right in the remaining part, effectively making it a full settlement; (2) include in the sulh agreement a clause of bara'ah (release/acquittal) from the claim on the remaining part. The commentary then discusses an internal Hanafi disagreement about whether the plaintiff's later claim on the rest would be heard (accepted by the court). Sheikh Najm al-Din al-Nasafi holds it would be heard because the ibra' from the specific right (ayn) is invalid, so the claim remains. But Sheikh al-Islam (al-Marghinani) and the Nihayah say it is not heard, and this is the Zahir al-Riwayah. Their reasoning: the ibra' in the sulh is primarily from the claim (da‘wa), and ibra' from a claim is valid. Even though ibra' from the specific right is invalid, the sulh is interpreted as a release from the claim, which suffices to bar later litigation. Thus, the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that the subsequent claim is not heard.

Key Terms
ابراء من العين (ibra’ min al-‘ayn): Release from a specific property right; considered invalid in Hanafi law because ownership cannot be transferred without exchange.
ابراء عن الدعوى (ibra’ ‘an al-da‘wa): Release from the right to sue; considered valid in Hanafi law because it terminates the claim without transferring property.
ظاهر الرواية (Zahir al-Riwayah): The authoritative transmitted rulings from Imam Muhammad's six books; the most relied-upon source in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in Hanafi, if a plaintiff claims a specific house and makes sulh on one room only (without adding anything or mentioning bara'ah), can he later sue for the rest of the house?
A) Yes, because the ibra' from the room is invalid
B) No, because the ibra' from the claim is valid
C) Yes, because the sulh is invalid
D) No, because he accepted part of his right

Key Takeaway
A partial sulh on a claimed specific property is invalid unless corrected by adding consideration for the remainder or including a release of the claim; the mufta bihi is that the release of the claim bars future litigation.

Settlement Consideration: Partial Reclamation of Claimed Property
Ruling on returning consideration when part of claimed property is reclaimed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for when part of a house is reclaimed after an unspecified settlement, and explain the rationale based on the possibility that the claim attaches to the remainder.

Core Idea
An unspecified claim survives partial reclamation; the consideration stands because the claim may be in the remainder.

This passage from the Hidayah addresses a practical scenario in property disputes: after an unspecified claim is settled, what happens if a third party reclaims part of the property? The ruling hinges on the distinction between partial and complete reclamation, grounded in the nature of an unspecified claim. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Hanafi fiqh preserves the validity of settlements when the subject matter is not fully lost.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
The scenario: A claimant asserts an unspecified right in a house (e.g., 'I have a right in this house' without specifying which part). A settlement (ṣulḥ) is reached, and the claimant receives consideration. Later, a third party establishes ownership over part of the house.
Unspecified claim is key – it is not tied to any particular portion.
2
State the Ruling for Partial Reclamation
When only part of the house is reclaimed, the claimant does not return any of the consideration. The settlement remains fully valid.
This is the central ruling of the passage.
3
Analyze the Rationale
Since the claim was unspecified, it could rationally correspond to the remaining portion of the house. The consideration is seen as given for that possibility. The settlement is not invalidated because a corresponding right still exists in the remainder.
The reasoning (ta'lil) is based on possibility (iḥtimāl) – the claim could be in the unclaimed part.
4
Contrast with Full Reclamation
If the entire house is reclaimed, there is no remaining part to which the claim could attach. The consideration becomes baseless and must be returned in full.
This contrast highlights the decisive factor: the existence of a remaining portion.
5
Note the Analogy to Sales
The passage refers to the chapter on sales for the same principle. In sales, if part of the sold item is reclaimed, the buyer may keep the rest and the price is apportioned. Here, because the claim was unspecified, the entire consideration stands – analogous to a sale where the specific portion is not identified.
The analogy (qiyas) reinforces the ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كسی نے اپنے حق کا دار میں دعویٰ کیا اور اس کی تعین نہیں کی اور اس سے صلح کر لی گئی پھر کچھ دار کا کوئی حقدار نکل آیا تو مدعی عوض سے کچھ واپس نہیں کرے گا
قَالَ وَإِنِ ادَّعَى حَقًّا فِي دَارٍ وَلَمْ يُبَيِّنَهُ فَصُولِحَ مِنْ ذلِكَ ثُمَّ اسْتَحَقَّ بَعْضَ الدَّارِ لَمْ يَرُدُّ شَيْئًا مِنَ الْعِوَضِ لِاَنَّ دَعْوَاهُ يَجُوزُ أَنْ يَكُونَ فِيمَا بَقِيَ بِخِلَافِ مَا إِذَا اسْتَحَقَ كُلَّهُ لِأَنَّهُ يَعْرَى الْعِوَضُ عِنْدَ ذَلِكَ عَنْ شَيْءٍ يُقَابِلُهُ فَرَجَعَ بِكُلِه عَلَى مَا قَدَّمْنَاهُ فِي الْبُيُوعِ
ترجمہ کسی نے دعوی کیا دار میں اپنے حق کا اور اس کی تعین نہیں کی۔ پس اس سے صلح کرلی گئی۔ پھر کچھ دار کا حقدار نکل آیا۔ تو مدعی عوض میں سے کچھ واپس نہیں کرے گا۔ کیونکہ ممکن ہے اس کا دعوی باقی ماندہ میں ہو۔ بخلاف اس کے جب پورا گھر مستحق نکالا۔ کیونکہ اس وقت خالی ہوگا۔ عوض ایسی چیز سے جو اس کے مقابل ہو۔ لہذا پورا عوض واپس لے گا۔ جیسا کہ ہم پہلے بیان کر چکے ۔ بیوع میں
تشریح قوله وان ادعی حقا مدعی نے کسی دار میں اپنے حق کا دعوی کیا اور اس کو بیان نہیں کیا۔ پھر دعوی سے صلح کرلی گئی۔ اس کے بعد دار کے کچھ حصہ میں استحقاق نکل آیا۔ تو مدعی عوض صلح میں سے کچھ واپس نہیں کرے گا۔ اس لئے کہ جب اس نے دار میں سے کوئی جگہ معین نہیں کی تو ہو سکتا ہے اس کا دعویٰ مکان کے باقی حصہ میں ہو۔ بخلاف اس کے جب استحقاق کل دار میں ثابت ہو کہ مدعی پورا عوض واپس کرے گا۔ کیونکہ اس صورت میں بدل صلح کسی چیز کے مقابلہ میں نہ ہوگا۔ لہذا مدعی علیہ اپنا پورا عوض واپس لے گا۔
Someone claimed a right in a house without specifying it, then a settlement was made from that. Then part of the house was established as belonging to someone else, the claimant will not return anything of the consideration, because his claim could be in what remains. Unlike if the whole house is established, because then the consideration becomes devoid of anything corresponding to it, so he takes back all of it, as we previously stated in the chapter of sales. Translation: Someone claimed a right in a house without specifying it. Then a settlement was made from it. Then a rightful claimant of part of the house appeared. The claimant will not return anything of the consideration. Because it is possible that his claim is in the remaining part. Unlike when the whole house is claimed, because then the consideration is empty of anything corresponding to it, so he takes back all of it. As we previously stated in the chapter of sales. Commentary: Explanation of 'and if he claimed a right': The claimant claimed a right in a house without specifying it. Then a settlement was made from the claim. Then a right to a part of the house appeared. The claimant will not return any of the settlement consideration. Because when he did not specify any specific place in the house, it is possible that his claim is in the remaining part of the house. Unlike when the right to the whole house is established, then the claimant will return all the consideration. Because in that case the settlement consideration is against nothing. So the defendant takes back his full consideration.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its reasoning from the Hidayah. It addresses the scenario where a person claims an unspecified right in a house, then reaches a settlement with the defendant, and later a third party establishes ownership over part of the house. The ruling: the settlement remains valid, and the claimant does not have to refund any of the consideration given. The reason is that since the claim was not tied to any specific part, it could rationally refer to the remaining portion of the house that is not reclaimed. Thus the consideration is given for that possible right. However, if the entire house is reclaimed by a true owner, the consideration becomes baseless—there is nothing left for it to correspond to—so the defendant can demand its full return. This principle is analogous to what was established earlier in the book of sales. The Urdu commentary affirms this and clarifies the distinction.

Key Terms
صلح (ṣulḥ): A contract to end a dispute, often by giving or receiving consideration, similar to a sale or exchange.
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): The establishment of a right of ownership in favor of a third party, causing the property to be taken from the current possessor.
عوض (ʿiwaḍ): The consideration or compensation given in exchange for the claim or right being settled.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the claimant not return any part of the consideration when part of the house is reclaimed, according to the passage?
A) Because the claim was specified for a particular part
B) Because the claimant already used the consideration
C) Because the claim could be in the remaining part of the house
D) Because the defendant waived the return

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a settlement on an unspecified claim remains fully valid even if part of the property is later reclaimed, because the claim may attach to the remainder.

Settlement for a Specific Piece of a Claimed House: Invalidity and Valid Stratagems
Ruling on partial settlement with remedy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on settling for a part of a claimed house, explain why it is invalid unless a valid device is used, and apply the two permitted devices (adding a dirham or including a quittance) to validate such a settlement.

Core Idea
A settlement on part of a claimed right is invalid unless the consideration includes an extra dirham or a quittance from the remaining claim.

This passage from the Hidayah addresses a subtle issue in sulh (settlement). When a claimant claims an entire house and then settles for only a specific piece, the settlement is inherently problematic because the claimant retains his claim to the rest—a contradiction. The Hanafi school provides two permissible lawful stratagems (hilah) to make such a settlement valid. Additionally, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute about whether the claimant can later sue for the remainder if the settlement was made without these devices. Understanding this ruling clarifies the importance of aligning settlement with the exact scope of the claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic ruling
The settlement for a specific piece of a claimed house is invalid. Note the reason: the claimant is taking part of his own right while still claiming the rest.
2
Understand the underlying principle
A settlement must fully resolve the claim. Taking part of the right without addressing the remainder leaves the claim partially active, which is contradictory and renders the settlement invalid.
3
Learn the two valid devices (hilah)
(a) Add an extra dirham to the settlement price so it covers the remaining right; (b) Include a quittance (barā’ah) from the claim on the remaining part.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
After an invalid settlement (without the devices), can the claimant sue for the rest? According to one narration (Shaikh Najm al-Din), yes, because the quittance from the actual thing is invalid. According to the zahir al-riwayah (preferred), no, because the quittance from the claim is valid even if the quittance from the actual thing is invalid.
5
Apply the preferred ruling
The mufta bihi is that the settlement is invalid unless a device is used, and if it is invalid, the claimant cannot later sue for the rest.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَلَوِ ادَّعَى دَارًا فَصَالَحَ عَلَى قِطْعَةٍ مِنْهَا لَمْ يَصِحُ الْصُّلْحُ لَانَّ مَا قَبَضَهُ عَيْنِ حَقِهِ وَهُوَ عَلَى دَعْوَاهُ فِي الْبَاقِي وَالْوَجْهُ فِيهِ اَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ إِمَّا أَنْ يَزِيدَ دِرْهَمًا فِي بَدَلِ الصُّلْحِ فَيَصِيرُ ذلِكَ عِوَضًا عَنْ حَقِهِ فِيمَا بَقِيَ أَوْ يَلْحَقَ بِهِ ذِكْرُ الْبَرَاءَةِ عَنْ دَعْوَى الْبَاقِي.
ترجمہ اگر دعوی کیا دار کا پس صلح کر لی اس کے ایک قطعہ پر تو صحیح نہ ہوگی ۔ کیونکہ مدعی نے جس پر قبضہ کیا ہے وہ اس کے عین میں سے ہے اور وہ باقی میں اپنے دعوئی پر ہے اور اس کے جواز میں دو باتوں میں سے ایک ہے یا تو بدل صلح میں ایک درہم بڑھا دے تا کہ یہ اس کے باقی دار کے حق کا عوض ہو جائے یا عوض کے ساتھ باقی دار کے دعوئی سے برأت کا ذکر لاحق کر دے۔
تشریح قوله و لوادعی دارا .... اگر مدعی نے دار کا دعویٰ کیا اور مدعلی علیہ نے اس کے ایک قطعہ پر صلح کر لی۔ مثلاً اس کے کسی ایک معین کرے پر تو صلح صحیح نہ ہوگی۔ کیونکہ مدعی نے جو کچھ قبضہ کیا ہے وہ اس کے عین حق میں سے بعض ہے اور باقی دار میں وہ اپنے دعوئی پر باقی ہے۔ پھر اگر مدعی باقی دار کے دعوئی پر بعینہ قائم کرے تو مسموع ہوگا یا نہیں۔ سوشیخ نجم الدین نسفی نے شرح کافی میں کہا ہے کہ مسموع ہوگا۔ کیونکہ اس نے اپنا بعض حق وصول پایا ہے۔ اور باقی سے ابراء کیا ہے اور ابراء من العین باطل ہے۔ تو اس کا وجود و عدم برابر ہوا۔ اور شیخ الاسلام نے اپنی شرح میں ذکر کیا ہے کہ مسموع نہ ہوگا۔ اس کو صاحب نہایہ نے ظاہر الروایہ کہا ہے۔ وجہ یہ ہے کہ ابراء مین اور دعوئی دونوں سے ملاتی ہے اور ابراء عن الدعوئی صحیح ہے۔ گواابراء عن العین باطل ہے۔
قوله والوجه فيه .. يعنى قطعة دار صلح حیح ہونے کا حیلہ یہ ہے کہ یا تو عوض صلح پر ایک درہم زیادہ کر دے تا کہ باقی دار کا عوض ہو جائے یا عوض صلح کے ساتھ باقی دار کے دعوئی سے برات کا ذکر لاحق کر دے۔ بأن يقول برنت من دعوای في هذه الدار تو باقی سے برکت ہو جائے گی۔ لمصادفة البرأة الدعوى وهو صحيح
And if he claimed a house and settled on a piece of it, the settlement is not valid, because what he took is part of his actual right, and he is still on his claim regarding the rest. The proper way in it is one of two things: either he adds a dirham to the settlement price, so that becomes consideration for his right in what remains, or he appends a mention of quittance from the claim of the rest. Translation: If he claimed a house and settled on one piece of it, it will not be valid. Because what the claimant took is part of his own right, and he is still on his claim regarding the rest. To make it valid, one of two things: either he adds a dirham to the settlement price so that it becomes consideration for his right in the remaining house, or he appends to the consideration a mention of quittance from the claim of the rest. Commentary: Explanation of 'and if he claimed a house'... If the claimant claimed a house and the defendant settled on one piece of it, for example a specific room, the settlement is not valid. Because what the claimant took is part of his actual right, and he remains on his claim regarding the rest of the house. Then, if the claimant files a suit on the remaining claim, will it be heard? Shaikh Najm al-Din al-Nasafi said in Sharh al-Kafi that it will be heard, because he has received part of his right and has been quitted from the rest, and quittance from the actual right is invalid, so its existence and non-existence are equal. But Shaikh al-Islam mentioned in his commentary that it will not be heard, and Sahib al-Nihayah said this is the zahir al-riwayah. The reason is that the quittance pertains to both the actual thing and the claim, and quittance from the claim is valid even if quittance from the actual thing is invalid. His statement 'and the proper way in it' means the stratagem for the validity of the settlement on a piece of the house is either to add a dirham to the settlement price so it becomes consideration for the right in the rest, or to append to the settlement price a mention of quittance from the claim of the rest, such as saying 'I am quitted from my claim in this house,' then quittance from the rest will occur, because the quittance coincides with the claim, and it is valid.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with its remedy. The text from the Hidayah states: If a claimant claims an entire house and then settles for a specific piece of it, the settlement is invalid because he is taking part of what he claims while still claiming the rest—this is a contradiction. To make such a settlement valid, there are two permissible devices (hilah). First: the claimant can add an extra dirham to the settlement consideration. This extra amount then serves as countervalue for the remaining claim, effectively transforming the transaction into a sale of the piece plus a settlement of the rest. Second: the claimant can include a clause of quittance (barā’ah) from the remaining claim in the settlement contract, i.e., he declares that he relinquishes his claim to the rest. The commentary then discusses an ikhtilāf: if after such an invalid settlement the claimant sues for the rest, will his suit be heard? According to a narration from Shaikh Najm al-Dīn al-Nasafī, it would be heard because the quittance from the actual thing is invalid, so the claim remains. But according to the widely accepted (zāhir al-riwāyah) view cited by Shaikh al-Islām and Ṣāḥib al-Nihāyah, the suit will not be heard because the quittance is from both the thing and the claim, and quittance from the claim is valid. The preferred ruling is that the settlement is invalid unless one of the two devices is used. The explanation concludes by reiterating the two valid methods.

Key Terms
حیلہ (ḥīlah): A lawful stratagem or device to achieve a valid legal result while avoiding an otherwise invalid transaction.
برأت / براءت (barāʾah): Quittance or release from a claim; a declaration that the claimant has no more right against the defendant regarding that claim.
ظاہر الروایہ (Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah): The most authoritative and widely transmitted position in the Hanafi school, derived from the six core works of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybānī.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two valid devices (hilah) to make a settlement for a specific piece of a claimed house valid?
A) Increase the consideration by one dirham or include a quittance from the remaining claim
B) Decrease the consideration or get a witness
C) Give the piece as a gift or sell the rest
D) Cancel the settlement or go to court

Key Takeaway
A settlement for a specific piece of a claimed house is invalid unless the claimant adds an extra dirham to the consideration or includes a quittance from the remaining claim, and if invalid, the preferred view is that the claimant cannot later sue for the rest.

Three Categories of Claims Valid for Settlement (Ṣulḥ)
Enumeration of valid claim types for ṣulḥ with analogical reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three categories of claims for which ṣulḥ is valid in the Hanafi school and explain the underlying principle of interpreting ṣulḥ by the nearest analogous contract.

Core Idea
Settlement is valid for property, usufructs, and injuries — each interpreted by the contract it most resembles.

This passage from al-Hidayah classifies the types of claims that can be resolved through ṣulḥ (settlement). Rather than listing all possibilities, the author gives three broad categories and a key principle: ṣulḥ is interpreted by the contract closest to it in nature. Understanding this principle helps you extend the ruling to other similar cases. The commentary unpacks the reasoning and gives a practical example for usufructs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three claim categories
The text states that ṣulḥ is valid for claims of properties (amwāl), usufructs (manāfiʿ), and both intentional and unintentional injuries (jināyāt). List them clearly.
Injuries include claims for diyah or qisās.
2
Understand the analogy for each category
For properties, ṣulḥ is compared to sale because both involve transferring ownership of a tangible asset. For usufructs, ṣulḥ is compared to lease (ijārah) because both involve transferring the right to use. For injuries, the text does not specify a direct analogy but implies that settlement is valid because the claim can be compromised for compensation.
The principle 'aṣl' governs the interpretation: carry ṣulḥ on the nearest contract.
3
Apply the principle to validate a transaction
The principle states that to validate the contracting party's action as much as possible, we interpret ṣulḥ by the contract it most resembles. This is a 'stratagem' (ḥīlah) to uphold the settlement if possible.
Example: if someone settles a usufruct claim, treat it like a lease to determine validity.
4
Recognise the scope of jināyāt
Both intentional (ʿamd) and unintentional (khaṭaʾ) injuries are included. This means claims for blood money or retaliation can be settled by mutual agreement.
Settlement in criminal cases avoids the need for qisās and is encouraged.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَضْلٌ) وَالصُّلْحُ جَائِزٌ عَنْ دَعْوَى الْأَمْوَالِ لِأَنَّهُ فِى مَعْنَى الْبَيْعِ عَلَى مَامَرَّ وَالْمَنَافِعُ لِأَنَّهَا تُمْلَكُ بِعَقْدِ الْإِجَارَةِ فَكَذَا بِالصُّلْحِ وَالْأَصْلُ اَنَّ الصُّلْحَ يَجِبُ حَمْلُهُ عَلَى أَقْرَبِ الْعُقُودِ إِلَيْهِ وَأَشْبَهَهَا بِهِ اِخْتِيَالًا لِتَصْحِيح تَصَرُّفِ الْعَاقِدِ مَا أَمْكَنَ . قَالَ وَ يَصِحُ عَنْ جِنَايَةِ الْعَمَدِ وَالْخَطَاءِ
ترجمہ ( فصل) اور مسلح جائز ہے اموال کے دعوے سے۔ کیونکہ یہ بیچ کے معنی میں ہے۔ جیسا کہ گزر چکا۔ اور منافع کے دعوے سے کیونکہ منافع مملوک ہو جاتے ہیں۔ عقد اجارہ سے تو ایسے ہی صلح سے بھی اور اصل یہ ہے کہ صلح کو اس سے قریب تر عقد پر محمول کیا جائے اور اس پر جو اس سے زیادہ مشابہ ہو۔ بقد را مکان تصرف عاقد کی صحیح کا حیلہ کرنے کے لئے اور صحیح ہے۔ جنایت عمد اور جنابت خطاء کے دعوئی سے۔
تشریح قول فصل مقدمات و شرائط اور انواع کے بعد ان چیزوں کو بیان کر رہا ہے۔ جن کی طرف سے صلح جائز ہے۔ اور جن کی طرف سے صلح جائز نہیں۔ اگر مدعی مال کا دعوی کرے تو دعوی مال کی طرف سے صلح کرنا جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ یہ صلح بیع کے معنی میں ہے۔ (علی ما مر فی اول كتاب الصُّلْحُ ) تو جس چیز کی بیع جائز ہے اس کی جانب سے صلح بھی جائز ہوگی۔ نیز دعوئی منفعت کی طرف سے بھی صلح کر سکتا ہے۔ مثلا زید نے دعویٰ کیا کہ فلاں شخص نے میرے لئے اس مکان میں ایک سال تک رہنے کی وصیت کی ہے۔ ورثہ نے اس کو کچھ مال دے کر صلح کر لی تو صلح جائز ہے۔ کیونکہ عقد اجارہ کے ذریعہ سے منافع مملوک ہو جاتے ہیں تو صلح کے ذریعہ سے بھی مملوک ہو سکتے ہیں۔
(Section) And settlement is valid for claims of properties, because it is in the meaning of sale, as has passed. And for usufructs, because they are owned by the contract of lease, so too by settlement. And the principle is that settlement must be carried upon the nearest contract to it and the most similar to it, as a stratagem to validate the transaction of the contracting party as much as possible. He said: And it is valid for claims of intentional and unintentional injury. Translation: (Section) Settlement is valid for claims of properties, because it is in the meaning of sale, as has passed. And for claims of usufructs, because usufructs become owned by the contract of lease, so similarly by settlement. And the principle is that settlement should be interpreted in light of the contract closest to it and most similar to it, as a stratagem to validate the transaction of the contracting party as much as possible. And it is valid for claims of intentional and unintentional injury. Commentary: Explanation: After the preliminaries, conditions, and types, he is now mentioning the things from which settlement is valid and from which it is not. If the claimant claims property, settlement from the claim of property is valid, because this settlement is in the meaning of sale. So anything whose sale is valid, settlement from it is also valid. Also, settlement can be made for claims of usufructs. For example, Zayd claimed that so-and-so bequeathed to him the right to live in this house for one year. The heirs settled with him by giving him some money. This is valid, because usufructs are owned by the contract of lease, so they can be owned by settlement as well.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of claims for which settlement (ṣulḥ) is valid. The text from the Hidayah states three categories: (1) Claims of properties (amwāl): Since ṣulḥ is akin to a sale, it is valid for any property that can be sold. (2) Claims of usufructs (manāfiʿ): Since usufructs can be owned through a lease contract, they can also be owned through a settlement, as the principle is to interpret ṣulḥ by the nearest analogous contract. (3) Claims for injuries (jināyāt), both intentional and unintentional: This includes claims for diyah (blood money) or qisās (retaliation) that can be settled by compromise. The commentary elaborates on the principle: to validate the contracting party's action as much as possible, ṣulḥ is interpreted according to the contract it most resembles—sale for property, lease for usufructs, etc. An example is given: if a legatee claims a right of residence and the heirs pay him off, it is valid as a settlement for the usufruct.

Key Terms
منافع (manāfiʿ): Usufructs; the right to use or benefit from something, such as residing in a house or riding an animal.
جنایت (jināyah): An injury or crime against a person, such as killing or wounding, which can be either intentional (ʿamd) or unintentional (khaṭaʾ).
اصل (aṣl): The fundamental principle or rule governing the interpretation of a contract, here stating that ṣulḥ is carried on the nearest contract to it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, which of the following is NOT a category for which ṣulḥ is valid?
A) Claims of properties (amwāl)
B) Claims of usufructs (manāfiʿ)
C) Claims of intentional injuries (jināyat al-ʿamd)
D) Claims of marriage (nikāḥ)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school allows settlement for property, usufruct, and injury claims, interpreting the settlement by the contract it most resembles to uphold its validity.

Validity of Sulh by a Ma'dhun Slave for Homicide
Ruling on validity of sulh by ma'dhun slave for qatl amd

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on whether a ma'dhun slave can make sulh for himself and for his own slave, and explain the legal reasoning (al-farq) using qiyas.

Core Idea
A ma'dhun slave's person is outside his trade capacity, so he cannot ransom himself, but his slave is within his trade.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Al-Hidayah regarding the capacity of a ma'dhun slave to settle homicide claims through sulh. The text applies a key distinction based on what falls within the slave's trading authority. We will analyze the ruling and its underlying analogy to understand the limits of a ma'dhun's contractual capacity in criminal matters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
State what the ma'dhun slave is doing (committing intentional homicide) and whether sulh is permissible for himself and for his own slave.
The ruling has two parts: not permissible for self, permissible for his slave.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is a qiyas (analogy) explained in the text. Identify the original cases (asl) and the new cases (far').
The text uses the analogy of a stranger (ajnabi) and the ma'dhun's own ability to sell his slave.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand the distinguishing factor (al-farq) that makes the two rulings different: whether the subject (his person or his slave) is part of his trade capital.
His own person is not part of his trade (tijara), so he cannot dispose of it by sale or ransom. His slave is part of his trade.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Although the text does not mention opposition, other schools may have different views on the ma'dhun's capacity. Be aware that this is a mukhtalaf fih issue across madhahib.
The cross-madhhab status is given as mukhtalaf_fih.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position in the Hanafi school.
There is no intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قَالَ وَإِذَا قَتَلَ الْعَبْدُ الْمَأْذُونُ لَهُ رَجُلًا عَمَدًا لَمْ يَجُزْ لَهُ أَنْ يُصَالِحَ عَنْ نَفْسِهِ وَإِنْ قَتَلَ عَبْدَلَهُ رَجُلًا عَمَدًا فَصَالَحَ عَنْهُ جَازَ وَوَجْهُ الْفَرْق اَنْ رَقَبَتَهُ لَيْسَتْ مِنْ تِجَارَتِهِ وَلِهَذَا لَا يَمْلِكُ التَّصَرُّقَ فِيهَا بَيْعًا فَكَذَا استخلاصا بِمَال الْمَوْلَى وَصَارَ كَالْأَجْنَبِي أَمَّا عَبْدُهُ فَمِنْ تِجَارَتِهِ وَتَصَرُّفُه نَافِدٌ فِيْهِ بَيْعًا فَكَذَا اسْتِخْلَاصًا وَهَذَا لِاَنَّ الْمُسْتَحَقَّ كَالزَّائِلِ عَنْ مِلْكِهِ وَهذَا شِرَاؤُهُ فَيَمْلِكُهُ
He said: When a ma'dhun slave intentionally kills a man, it is not permissible for him to make sulh on behalf of himself. But if his slave intentionally kills a man, and he makes sulh on his behalf, it is permissible. The distinction is that his own person (raqaba) is not part of his trade; therefore he does not own the right to dispose of it by sale, and similarly he cannot redeem it with the master's property. He is like a stranger (ajnabi). As for his slave, he is part of his trade, and his disposition regarding him (by sale) is effective, so similarly redemption (istikhlas) is effective. This is because the one subject to claim (mustahaqq) is like one who has left his ownership, and this [sulh] is his purchase; thus he owns it.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This is a ruling from the Book of Sulh concerning a ma'dhun slave (a slave permitted to trade) who commits intentional homicide. The passage states that if such a slave kills a man intentionally, he is not permitted to make a sulh on his own behalf. However, if his own slave (i.e., a slave owned by the ma'dhun) commits intentional homicide, and the ma'dhun makes sulh on behalf of that slave, it is valid. The reason for the distinction (wajh al-farq) is that the ma'dhun's own person (raqaba) is not part of his trade capital (tijara); therefore he does not own the right to dispose of it by sale, and similarly he cannot redeem it with the master's money. In this respect, he is like a stranger (ajnabi) regarding his own person. As for his slave, that slave is part of his trade, and his disposition regarding the slave (such as sale) is valid, so likewise his redeeming the slave via sulh is valid. The reasoning is that when the slave becomes subject to the claim of retaliation (mustahaqq), it is as if he has left the ma'dhun's ownership, and paying sulh to redeem him is akin to purchasing him; therefore the ma'dhun has the right to purchase.

Key Terms
عبد مأذون (abd ma'dhun): A slave who is permitted by his master to engage in trade.
صلح (sulh): A settlement or compromise between two parties, often for payment to avoid liability or dispute.
استخلاص (istikhlas): Freeing or redeeming a slave from liability by paying compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why can a ma'dhun slave make sulh for his own slave but not for himself?
A) Because his own person is not part of his trade capital.
B) Because the slave is cheaper.
C) Because the master forbids it.
D) Because sulh is not allowed for homicide.

Key Takeaway
A ma'dhun slave's capacity for sulh is limited to what is within his trade capital; his own person is not part of his trade, so he cannot ransom himself.
